Menel started to ball his hands into fists. I had a moment of panic, and then‚Äî

A scruffy-looking man sluggishly wandered over.

‚ÄúShut it, blowhards.‚Äù

His few words silenced them.

The man had a beard, and I couldn‚Äôt guess his age. He seemed to be in good shape physically, but looked pretty spiritless. The cloak he was wearing was scorched, worn down, and covered in scratches. The sword sheath on his hip looked beaten up and like it had some alterations made to it. But more than any of that, what I paid the most attention to was his fingers.

They were covered in scars and dirt, and all his nails were clipped short. Once, while relating one of his former exploits, Blood had said to me:

‚Äî When you see a swordsman, look at his fingertips. Whenever there‚Äôs something inside you making you doubt, saying drawing your weapon is a bad idea, and you wanna know, do you listen to that voice or do you shut it up? You just look at his hands.

‚ÄúIt looks to me...‚Äù He spoke slowly. I guessed he wasn‚Äôt good with words. ‚ÄúLike you‚Äôre looking for madmen. You‚Äôre not interested in blowhards, who have manners and patience and a business smile, but not much skill. You want a bunch of crude shitheads who fear nothing. You want scum-of-the-earth madmen who will dice with death for a dumb idea.‚Äù

I nodded. I wasn‚Äôt planning on giving them poor compensation on purpose, but the fact remained that exterminating demons in a poor area like this was a dangerous and not very lucrative job. There were still some untouched ruins still remaining, but even those came with dangers, and I didn‚Äôt want people working for me under false pretenses.

Menel and I had both agreed that we should look for adventurers who were after honor, glory, and risk rather than adventurers who were only doing it for the money. And I‚Äôd heard that this ‚ÄúSteel Sword Inn‚Äù was where those kinds of people were based. So I replied:

‚ÄúThat‚Äôs exactly right. That‚Äôs why I chose this place.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou hear him? That‚Äôs what he wants! Mister Hero‚Äôs looking for madmen!‚Äù After he yelled this, a number of people who had been watching us from their tables rose to their feet.

‚ÄúTch. You Strider bastards,‚Äù one of the blowhards said. ‚ÄúIf you strike it rich out there, toss us a coin or two for once!‚Äù

All the people with attractive equipment, like the one who had first called out to me, lightly clicked their tongues and returned to their tables. I guessed they‚Äôd been hoping for something they could profit from, and if that‚Äôs not what this was, they were clearly not interested. It was only natural that some people would put their livelihoods first and foremost.

Those who now approached me, on the other hand, were largely uncouth people with dirty gear and a prickly manner. Most of their equipment was covered in beast hide, and they had been drinking their booze out of beast horns. These were people who would hardly give a second‚Äôs consideration to safe and secure jobs, like being a merchant‚Äôs bodyguard. They were ruffians to the core who liked the flames of their lives to burn hot, stoked with fighting, risk, and adventure.

Yes‚Äîthey were people like Blood!

‚ÄúWhat‚Äôre you looking for in Beast Woods?‚Äù one asked.

‚ÄúRuins or open air?‚Äù asked another.

‚ÄúI don‚Äôt do small shit.‚Äù

I deliberately gave them all a fearless grin. ‚ÄúThe boss of the demons.‚Äù

When I said that, some of the adventurers went silent for a moment. I cast my eyes over them all. ‚ÄúThe leader of the demons that are running wild in the western part of Beast Woods. He‚Äôs thought to have beasts under him. He is our target.‚Äù

‚ÄúBig one...‚Äù the bearded man who had first spoken to me said, thinking aloud.

‚ÄúYes, it is,‚Äù I replied.

‚ÄúLocation‚Äôs unclear... It‚Äôd take some work to even find him.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou‚Äôre absolutely right.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd if we get ambushed while we‚Äôre searching, they‚Äôll kill us in a blink.‚Äù

‚ÄúI imagine so.‚Äù

‚ÄúLong story short‚Äîthis sounds like a stupid, full of risk, fun-as-hell adventure.‚Äù He laughed, as if at death. ‚ÄúIf there‚Äôs a spot for me, I‚Äôm in. Just need food and a place to sleep and I‚Äôm good. If there‚Äôs some pocket change in it for me, even better.‚Äù

‚ÄúMe too.‚Äù ‚ÄúAnd me.‚Äù Other voices quickly followed, saying the same thing.

‚ÄúOf course. You will have them. And payment as well.‚Äù

A cheer rose from the group.

‚ÄúBut before that,‚Äù I said.

‚ÄúWhat?‚Äù

I smiled and extended my hand to the man.

‚ÄúWould you all tell me your names? I‚Äôm Will. William G. Maryblood.‚Äù

‚ÄúReystov.‚Äù

Something that Bee had once said resurfaced in my mind.

‚Äî Oh right, have to pick something. Of the recent songs... Reystov the Penetrator is overplayed right now...

‚ÄúThe Penetrator?‚Äù

‚ÄúGet called that,‚Äù the bearded adventurer replied gruffly.

‚óÜ

Those days in Whitesails went by quickly.

‚ÄúI, the Duke of Southmark, Ethelbald Rex Southmark, confer the honor of knighthood upon thee.‚Äù

The temple‚Äôs church was majestic. His Excellency was standing at the far end, with crowds of attendees on either side. Beside him was the vice-bishop, who would bestow his blessing upon me. He had thin, kind-looking eyes and a gentle face shape that was quite memorable.

I walked slowly towards them.

To tell the truth, I wanted to have Bishop Bagley bless me since he‚Äôd done so much for me already, but when I asked him, he refused point-blank. He said it would cause him problems to publicly show himself being pious and having a deep connection with the gods; during his many negotiations, it was important that he be able to lead the other person into thinking he might do something a pious priest never would. He was very thorough about it, even going so far as to offer half-hearted prayers in front of others and then pray anew when he was alone.

It was a real shame. I had expressed this to the vice-bishop, and he concurred with me, saying that he, too, was disappointed such a great man would go unknown to the world.

I found the vice-bishop to be a very nice person.

I reached where I was meant to stand for the ceremony. The duke took a sword from where it rested on the altar and said, ‚ÄúMay he become the guardian of this temple, of the needy, and of all who place trust in the good gods, and stand against the gods of evil and their atrocities.‚Äù

The sword was handed to the vice-bishop, then passed to me. I put the sword into the sheath that had been arranged for me beforehand, and then, following the ceremony as I had been instructed, I drew and re-sheathed it three times. The clear sound of the sword‚Äôs motion against the sheath echoed around the church.

The duke continued his speech. ‚ÄúTo you, the one who will now become a knight: You must defend the teachings of the good gods and protect the temple, the needy, and all those who pray and work in earnest.‚Äù

I dropped to one knee, adjusted my hold on the sword so I was holding the sheath with both hands, and presented him the handle. His Excellency drew the sword, and with the side of the blade, he lightly tapped my shoulders three times.

The sword was then returned to me. I accepted it, stood, and placed it back in its sheath, the sound once more filling the church.

The vice-bishop used the blessing of Sanctification, and a holy aura filled the air. ‚ÄúI beseech thee, my guardian deity Enlight, god of knowledge, that through thee our voices may be heard! May the blessing of Gracefeel, god of the flame, be with this man always and forever!‚Äù

The god of knowledge, Enlight‚Äîhe was the aged god with one eye, the god of learning, who could perceive both what could be seen as well as what could not.

‚ÄúHold fast to your oath, respect your god‚Äôs teachings, and protect the vulnerable. May you be a light unto the world!‚Äù

He shouted the end with arms spread wide, and cheers and applause erupted from the crowd.

‚ÄúMay you be a light unto the world!‚Äù

‚ÄúGod bless the birth of our new knight!‚Äù

‚ÄúMay the light shine on the frontier!‚Äù

‚ÄúBlessed be the knight of the torch!‚Äù

‚ÄúLong live the Paladin!‚Äù

And very quickly after that, the place was taken over by festivities. To loud cheers, all the influential, powerful people present gave generous donations to the crowds. This decoration ceremony had provided an excuse for a big handout to those who had suffered damage from the wyvern. That alone had made it worth it, I felt.

An enormous feast was held. It was a whole-city event. Wrestling matches were arranged as entertainment. After I won by fall against five people in a row, my satisfied grin was the last straw for a whole group of knights who surrounded me and made me suffer the same defeat.

‚ÄúWe beat the Wyvern Killer!‚Äù they shouted out gleefully, laughing their heads off.

‚ÄúYou cheaters!‚Äù I laughed with them. ‚ÄúMenel, Menel! Come on, you fight me, too!‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat?! No, feck off!‚Äù

As usual, Menel didn‚Äôt want to join in on party stuff. I dragged him out.

‚ÄúOh! You‚Äôre the Paladin‚Äôs servant, uh...‚Äù

‚ÄúHe‚Äôs not my servant, he‚Äôs my friend!‚Äù

‚ÄúWe‚Äôre not friends!‚Äù Menel shot back.

‚ÄúR-Right...‚Äù

Bee was cheerfully singing my story. She said something about how much money she was raking in. I was too embarrassed to listen.

Tonio and Reystov seemed to be taking advantage of the feast to make connections with all kinds of new people. They never missed a trick.

The festivities continued into the night.

And that was how I became the paladin of this faraway land.

‚óÜ

All kinds of things continued moving forward at an incredible pace. After all the merrymaking was over, I put in a formal request to Ethel and the vice-bishop and received permission to go demon-hunting. Ostensibly, I was a single priest doing some independent charity work, but I had the backing of proper authority and power. The scale of what I was now involved in was incredible, but this was the smoothest way to get things done. If there was any trouble from now on, it would probably be necessary to demonstrate my allegiance to the bishop and the duke, but that was the price to pay for my new authority. I couldn‚Äôt imagine that anything would develop that quickly, so I decided to think about that later.

Bishop Bagley supplied me with a number of priests, including Anna, who could use benediction and was versed in all kinds of ceremonies, from celebrations to funerals. The priests all had a wealth of experience, reliably making up for the areas where I was lacking. I felt so indebted to Bishop Bagley that I didn‚Äôt know if I‚Äôd ever be able to fully pay him back.

Tonio seemed to have used the festivities surrounding the knighting ceremony to collect donations and contributions for my work. He‚Äôd gathered together lots of carts and wagons, agricultural and workshop tools, textiles, consumables, seeds for commercial crops, and animals cured of their injuries and diseases. And he‚Äôd found workers to manage them all.

Tonio laughed and said, ‚ÄúI might be able to start up my own trading company soon with all of this.‚Äù I nodded enthusiastically, thinking that I would very much like him to do that, and he said with a mischievous smile, ‚ÄúI can look forward to receiving your business, then.‚Äù

I put the ruffian adventurers I‚Äôd contracted to work as convoys guarding our purchases, and we all returned to Beast Woods. That was the start of yet another hectic period.

I dropped by each of the villages we‚Äôd visited on the way to Whitesails, giving them a further round of medical treatment and having Anna help me organize religious festivals. With help from Tonio, I lent out draft animals and various kinds of tools in exchange for providing us shelter; I also allowed the villagers to buy them from us and pay in installments.

When there were reports of demons or beasts, I had Reystov and some of the other adventurers form a party and go out to hunt them. Reystov in particular was incredibly skilled; by and large, the corpses of the beasts he brought back had been penetrated straight through their most vulnerable spots, just as his nickname boasted.

Just out of curiosity, I asked him, ‚ÄúCan you do that to a wyvern?‚Äù and he replied gruffly, ‚ÄúIf it‚Äôs within sword‚Äôs reach.‚Äù

That wasn‚Äôt all. When there were disputes between the villages, I mediated them. When there were crimes in a village, I asked for the help of the priests and we held trials for those involved, so the matter would be solved as fairly as possible. I hadn‚Äôt originally been planning to do so much, but Tom, the elder of the village I‚Äôd first helped when a dispute arose, asked me if I‚Äôd help him out again since I‚Äôd done it once before. I couldn‚Äôt say no. And as my reputation spread, other villages also started to request my help to handle disputes that were getting out of hand, and I found myself with more and more to do. That‚Äôs how I ended up I traveling all over the place, doing all kinds of work.

When I heard about a village I hadn‚Äôt come into contact with, I got someone from the village we were currently in to introduce us and forge a connection, and then I‚Äôd do the same thing there. Bee really came in handy for making friendly first contact with unknown villages and whenever we had some message we needed spread around. I got the feeling I was paying for that with all the embellished stories she was also spreading about me. Maybe I needed to think of it as the cost of doing business.

Of course, if I kept on doing this kind of thing, I‚Äôd quickly find myself in debt in the literal sense. But while that was true, the livestock and agricultural tools that I‚Äôd lent out and sold off to the villages hadn‚Äôt gone anywhere; they remained in the village as valuable community property, and were visibly accelerating the villages‚Äô development and production. These villages were essentially nothing more than places where vagrants had gathered together, so for many of them, just a plow, an iron axe, and a hoe would be tremendously valuable additions. And if we made that a plow and a horse to go with it, and threw in a ten-piece set of metal farming implements and other tools, work efficiency would jump up dramatically. If that improved, there would be more fields giving greater yields. With greater yields, the people would be able to repay their debts to us, and they‚Äôd become able to afford to purchase goods.

In parallel, I and the adventurers would be clearing Beast Woods of the dangerous demons and beasts, making the area safer. As the area became safer, merchants would be able to go to and from the villages without the need for heavy escorting, which would lead to a burst of commercial activity. The Fertile Kingdom‚Äôs authority didn‚Äôt extend here anyway, so it wasn‚Äôt like there was going to be a toll for them to pay. They could do business here freely. And with more merchants coming in and out, the villagers could buy things with currency. Their improved production capacity should lead to them obtaining all kinds of things with money. Before long, an increasing number of those places would start dabbling in commercial crops in their desire for cash, anticipating demand for them from the city. And once money and goods started changing hands, neighboring areas would naturally become more interconnected for the sake of commerce. Access and transportation would improve. This was what Gus lovingly called ‚Äúliving money,‚Äù money that moved around and made itself useful.

‚ÄúAnd then one day, perhaps we can climb back out of the red,‚Äù Tonio said, while doing some estimates on his abacus. ‚ÄúThat is, if you and I are both still alive.‚Äù That was definitely something I was aiming for, living at least long enough to get back out of the red.

Of course, we had only just gotten the ball rolling, and not everything was going according to plan. Often, people would try to engineer things so they‚Äôd keep all the profit for themselves, or intentionally default on everything they‚Äôd borrowed. I tried to contain situations like that as much as I could. Usually, this was by getting help from Anna and the other priests, who knew about law and how to persuade people, but some of the scarier-looking adventurers were also effective deterrents. Fortunately, in the short span of time that we were involved, there wasn‚Äôt anyone dangerous enough to try something too disruptive. Even if a person like that was lurking somewhere, it was probably natural that they hadn‚Äôt tried, in a way. If you did something like that in place like this, you‚Äôd find yourself surrounded and beaten until you became tree food.

Mary had once told me, ‚ÄúThe greatest trap one can fall into when trying to do something good is to make the mistake of thinking that because you are acting with a good goal in mind, you are bound to get results.‚Äù Even if you decide to do something good, the people around you won‚Äôt lend you their help unconditionally, nor will the gods bless you with protection. Results come only by setting a reasonable goal and using appropriate methods to achieve it. And so, Mary had told me, the most important thing is to be practical and realistic. I took advice from everything Gus had taught me about money and regularly consulted with Menel, Bee, Tonio, Reystov, Anna, and the elders of villages all over, all of whom were very familiar with this world and its customs.

And together, we moved things forward. We went all over Beast Woods, back and forth, and as the winter transitioned into spring, I started to get the feeling that I was seeing more smiles in the villages. I felt as if there were now slightly fewer people who had no idea what tomorrow would even bring, who would wear gloomy or expressionless faces, or who would lose it completely and go off the rails. Perhaps that was what triggered the memory of something that Gus had once lectured me on.

‚Äî If you want something done, you don‚Äôt have to use magic. You just buy the tools you need or hire some people. Reshaping the terrain is a powerful piece of magic, but if you‚Äôve got money, you can just hire laborers and workmen to do construction for you instead. Make no mistake, the ability to earn money and make it work for you is just as important as magic!

‚ÄúYeah...‚Äù I finally understood what Gus had been getting at‚Äîand he was right. Even when he said something that made you twist your head, Gus‚Äôs lessons were always right. Making people smile and giving them hope... It felt like magic greater than magic itself.

‚óÜ

Under a canopy set up in a vacant lot I‚Äôd rented from one of the villages, I was giving Pale Moon an inspection, checking that the neck of the blade and the metal collar were in good shape. As I idly wondered whether summer might start to make itself felt soon, a gruff voice called out to catch my attention. I looked up as Reystov came over.

‚ÄúPip‚Äôs party‚Äôs not come back,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúThey were the ones searching the west.‚Äù

Pip... If I remembered correctly, he was a young lad who‚Äôd come from some farm. He‚Äôd been in a party with two other men, Harvey and Brennan. ‚ÄúHow long have they been gone?‚Äù

‚ÄúThey said they‚Äôd take ten days at the longest. They‚Äôre over by two already. And those guys have skills.‚Äù He was obviously implying that something must have happened for them to be this late.

‚ÄúAll right. We‚Äôll go out and search for them.‚Äù I thought for a moment about who should go. It was possible that there had been some kind of accident, or they‚Äôd been attacked by wild beasts. But there was also the remote possibility that Pip‚Äôs party had been spotted by the demons‚Äô lookouts. In which case, we‚Äôd need party members with combat skills. Also, to be absolutely sure we could follow their trail, we‚Äôd need a hunter or ranger skilled in tracking.

‚ÄúMe, Menel, and you are definites. Also, whichever two parties you think are most skilled at forest exploration, I‚Äôd like to merge those into our search party as well. Are you okay with that?‚Äù

Reystov nodded to say he was happy with my suggestion. ‚ÄúI‚Äôll get everyone together right away.‚Äù

Our party members gathered quickly in the village square. I explained the situation to them simply. I could talk about the details once we were on the move.

‚ÄúPip and the others are two days past their return date. We‚Äôre going to go searching for them, but there‚Äôs a possibility that there‚Äôs been more than just an accident. ‚ÄúWh‚Äî?!‚Äù

I nodded, a little surprised at its lucid question. And if that was its answer to me, then I could be virtually certain: the demons‚Äô objective was neither deep nor far from here.

The demons taking over this base had all been part of the greater plan.

So, for the demons, Beast Woods was a place that had to remain ravaged. It had to be a crucible of conflict, poverty, and disorder.

They couldn‚Äôt allow people to set their sights on the south.

‚ÄúIn the name of Gracefeel, I will destroy you all.‚Äù

‚ÄúA false impression.‚Äù

‚ÄúYes. You see‚Äî‚Äù

‚óÜ

As the chimera fought to shake off the earth and stone clinging to it, I took my chance. Screaming a war cry, I relentlessly gouged into it with the blade of my spear. On the other side of it, Menel had shot an arrow towards one of its goat eyes.

Being a multi-headed beast meant it had multiple brains, and if each issued a different command for a different reflex action, it was obvious that the body receiving them would become confused. This beast was simply unnatural as a living creature.

I stabbed it repeatedly with my spear, twisted it in the wounds and making it bleed. I avoided it when it tried to bite me, and deflected its heads with my shield.

There was no need to win cleanly in a single strike. I just needed to fight normally, and win by being better. I didn‚Äôt have any spectacular tricks up my sleeve, or any ultimate moves. ‚ÄúOne down!‚Äù

There was just the demidragon and lion heads left, plus the venomous snake tail‚Äîno, that was already gone. Menel had found an opening to sever it with a spell. He was quick.

‚ÄúIt is an accursed dragon power, but you leave me no choice!‚Äù

Dragon? I thought, but had no more time to think about it. The chimera‚Äôs veins turned black. Its muscles swelled, becoming misshapen and even thicker than before, and miasma gushed from its entire body.

‚ÄúThis guy, too?!‚Äù Menel spat out, infuriated.

‚ÄúMenel, stand back and stand by.‚Äù

‚ÄúI‚Äôll beat him down now.‚Äù

‚óÜ

Back when I fought the wyvern, I‚Äôd avoided this by choking it just before it got the chance. But this time, I‚Äôd only moments ago leaped backwards. Telling myself anything that came to mind to muster up my courage, I held my shield up in front of my face and charged forward. I closed the distance in less than a second and slammed my shield into the demidragon head‚Äôs wide-open mouth.

I felt the all-too-real sensation of slamming into flesh. Several fangs broke off in different directions, and the fire breath stopped. The chimera stiffened for a moment. Maybe it hadn‚Äôt expected me to come straight at it through the flames.

‚Äú‚ÄòGnomes, gnomes, form a fist! Clench your hands and strike the foe!‚Äô‚Äù Menel cast Stone Fist. There were a lot of small stones scattered all over the ground. They leaped up like a rising fist, and pounded into the chimera‚Äôs vast belly.

The chimera let out a cry of intense anguish. As it writhed in agony, I thrust my spear through its demidragon neck, finishing off its second head. The chimera flailed and threw its front legs around me, trying to grab me. My way forward was completely blocked by the lion head, and left and right were closed off by the wide reach of its front legs as they closed in. There was nowhere for me to escape.

‚ÄúAcceleratio!‚Äù

Except up.

I leaped almost directly upwards. The Word of Acceleration was one of my favorites, but I hadn‚Äôt used it even once in this chimera battle until now. The ground was just too unsuited for it. Unlike Menel, who had completely ignored the problem by using his elemental powers to run everywhere, I hadn‚Äôt been using any particularly fast maneuvers this entire time. So this move was one the chimera had no knowledge of.

It lost sight of me for an instant and then, realizing what had happened, it looked up‚Äîand was momentarily blinded by the light of the sun.

‚Äú‚ÄòGnomes, gnomes, take his feet! Harden, bind, and nail him down!‚Äô‚Äù Simultaneously, Menel cast Hold, with perfect timing.

I roared, and with the sun at my back and Pale Moon in my hands, I let my fall give me momentum, and drove the spear down into its lion head.

I felt it sink through skin, muscle, and bone, and then the impact of my landing. I immediately tried to pull the spear out and leap away, but it was stuck. I had an instant of panic, and I released the spear and leaped back without it. Then I realized. The chimera had already expired.

It was no wonder I couldn‚Äôt pull out Pale Moon; it had sunk all the way through the chimera‚Äôs lion head, and was stuck in the ground on the other side.

‚óÜ

I turned around to see that the beast extermination was almost over as well. Most of the beasts were already sprawled on the ground, and even those that were still running about looked badly wounded. It didn‚Äôt look like the others needed any help.

‚ÄúWe won!‚Äù

‚ÄúNice!‚Äù

Menel and I high-fived. It made a satisfying sound.

This hadn‚Äôt been the kind of magnificent victory I‚Äôd scored against the god of undeath. It wasn‚Äôt a triumph of the underdog against the obvious favorite; it was an ordinary, routine win. But even so, I thought that was fine. If grueling battles like the one I‚Äôd fought against the god of undeath were a regular occurrence, that would be unbearable. And besides‚Äîwe still had enemies ahead of us.

‚ÄúMoving on!‚Äù

‚ÄúYa!‚Äù

Wary of traps, we stepped inside the ruins of the monastery.

The inside was being kept illuminated by magic, which had probably come from the demons. The place had been stripped of its former stillness and holiness and transformed into a place of hideous rituals and research. We ran down long corridors, passing room after wide-open room, taking side glances at their contents: blood, meat, guts, beastlings preserved in strange fluid, magic circles in ghastly colors of paint.

They had to already know about our assault. It was possible that the demons who were controlling this base would choose to flee, and if that happened, the same thing might repeat somewhere else. We had to finish them off here, and both I and Menel were determined to do whatever it took to make it happen.

We burst out of the corridor. Our view opened up.

We were in the monastery‚Äôs chapel.

It was a very spacious place where sculptures of the gods were enshrined, and reminded me of the temple in the city of the dead that had once been my home.

But the several statues of the gods lined up at the back of the chapel had the details of their faces scraped off, just like the ones I‚Äôd seen previously in that village. The text honoring the gods, which should have been on the wall, had been scraped away. In its place were Words of praise for the god of dimensions, written large in darkened blood in a unearthly style that was nauseating to look at. And there was Dyrhygma‚Äôs crest, featuring arms grasping the eternal cycle.

It was a demonic ritual site.

‚ÄúTook you long enough.‚Äù

A quiet voice echoed about the chapel.

When Menel and I heard that voice, our eyes bulged. There was a bearded man there looking at us, wearing a scratched-up cloak and holding a sword. And on his face he was wearing a grin the likes of which I‚Äôd never seen.

No way...

‚ÄúRey...stov...?‚Äù

‚ÄúYeah.‚Äù

Unbelievable.

How on earth‚Äî

How‚Äî

His grin widened as he watched me try to make sense of this.

‚ÄúYou owe me ten gold coins,‚Äù he said cheerily, and pointed to the body of a large demon lying dead on the ground.

The demon, which was turning slowly to dust before my eyes, looked like a cross between a bat, a wolf, and a person. I had a memory of learning from Gus that these demons, called belalgors, were Commander demons considered to be extremely powerful for their rank. And this belalgor‚Äôs chest had been penetrated with a single, beautifully clean strike.

Yeah... so... in short... what had happened here was...

‚ÄúYou beat us to it?!‚Äù

‚ÄúNo way! How the hell did you do that?!‚Äù

‚ÄúWent around. You guys were fighting the chimera. Thanks for that, by the way. Made it nice and easy.‚Äù

Reystov had made his way inside the monastery while we were desperately fighting the chimera. He‚Äôd hunted down every last demon here and stuck them all with his sword; and then, here in the chapel, he‚Äôd confronted the belalgor who had been the unifying force for this base, and stuck him, too.

Of course, it couldn‚Äôt have been as simple as he‚Äôd made it sound.

‚ÄúReystov the Penetrator, my god... You live up to your name.‚Äù

He clearly hadn‚Äôt been given it for nothing.

‚ÄúNo wonder you get all the glory... You are way too good at getting the jump on people.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou need to be to land the real tough ones,‚Äù Reystov answered, sounding for once like he was in a good mood.

From the entrance to the monastery, I heard a jumble of noise and voices.

‚ÄúOkay, now watch yourselves! Who knows what traps are in there!‚Äù

‚ÄúWe‚Äôll be first in! Hope you‚Äôre all ready!‚Äù

‚ÄúFor honor and glory! And ten gold coins!‚Äù

They sounded pretty pumped up. I laughed weakly.

It was a pretty unsatisfying conclusion, but for some reason, I felt that was fitting.

‚óÜ

Under the brightness of the summer sun, long grass swayed in a pleasant breeze.

‚ÄúYeeeaaahhh!‚Äù

‚ÄúHere‚Äôs to victory!‚Äù

The field was full of adventurers clapping each other on the shoulder and raising their horns in toast.

There had been a lot to deal with in the immediate aftermath of the battle, but we‚Äôd sorted most of it out, and were attempting to head back to Whitesails. However, the enormous number of beast heads and demon dust we were carrying with us caused roars of celebration in each village that we passed through. Casks were brought out, and parties started in middle of the day. The ale poured into the horns tasted refreshing under the early summer sunshine.

All the villages had that same kind of party mood. The adventurers, too, were enjoying the feeling of release after their life-or-death battle, and raising a racket in every place they went. The trip back was very lively.

We were soon able to join back up with Bee and Tonio, whom we‚Äôd unfortunately left behind some time ago.

With his usual, soft expression, Tonio congratulated me on a job well done. It was thanks to his help that I was able to provide dozens of adventurers with the supplies they needed, and get them all working together. He was always lending me his subtle and modest support when things began to look shaky, the time after I beat the wyvern being the perfect example. I wanted to find some way to thank him sometime soon.

Bee jumped at me and Menel with her usual brightness. She pestered us repeatedly to talk more about our adventure, and ran ideas for her tales by us. And then, clenching a fist, she declared, ‚ÄúLooks like I need to get Menel included in this one!‚Äù

‚ÄúOh, shit! No!‚Äù The look on Menel‚Äôs face completely changed as he suddenly realized that he should have been paying attention to this conversation as well.

‚ÄúAwww, why not?!‚Äù

‚ÄúThis weirdo might be happy to grin like a goofball while everyone gawks at him, but I‚Äôm not!‚Äù

‚ÄúMeanie pants! I can tell you‚Äôre planning to follow your darling Will down the path of heroes anyway, so put up with it, mister grouchy!‚Äù

‚ÄúShut up! And he‚Äôs not my darling!‚Äù

‚ÄúHe so is! Oh! I know! I‚Äôll make your title Meneldor the Beautiful!‚Äù

‚ÄúThen all the poets are gonna add some twist, like turning me into a woman!‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd then you can be Will‚Äôs girlfriend!‚Äù

‚ÄúDammit, if you agree with me, don‚Äôt do it!‚Äù

Everyone burst out laughing watching the two of them running around. I laughed, too. As usual, Bee was cheerful, spoke her mind, and had a way of making all my worries seem completely stupid. Watching her really made me think: Yeah, there are other ways to go through life! You can be pretty carefree, if you want!

Incidentally, let me add that as a result of Menel‚Äôs fierce objections, his nickname was revised from ‚ÄúMeneldor the Beautiful‚Äù to ‚ÄúMeneldor of Swift Wings.‚Äù

The excitement of taking down a powerful enemy seemed to have worn off for Reystov; he had returned to his normal, lethargic self, taking tiny sips of his drink with a sullen, dull look on his face. He was the kind of guy who didn‚Äôt make a big thing of himself. He had a kind of subdued coolness.

The finesse he had with that sword was very impressive, too. He‚Äôd killed that demon in a single thrust. I asked him if we could train together sometime, and he gave me a silent nod. I hoped my eyes would be good enough to spot the secret to his stabbing technique. I badly wanted to steal it.

I looked around at the villagers. Now that the threat from demons and beasts had eased off, their faces were looking a lot sunnier.

‚ÄúEverything‚Äôs gotten so... lively.‚Äù

Only about half a year had passed since the days when I‚Äôd lived in that city of the dead with just me, Blood, Mary, and Gus. What a merry circle of people I‚Äôd surrounded myself with in just that short span of time.

‚ÄúIt‚Äôll get even livelier,‚Äù Menel said from beside me. ‚ÄúThe beasts and demons are much less of a threat now. More people are gonna come to the south, looking for a new world down here. I bet it‚Äôll cause some problems, too...‚Äù He was looking philosophically into the distance. ‚ÄúGonna have to live with it, I guess.‚Äù

‚ÄúYeah. You‚Äôre right. Wonder what I‚Äôm going to do now.‚Äù

Menel tilted his head when he heard that. ‚ÄúNow?‚Äù

‚ÄúYeah, I mean, what next. Of course, if there‚Äôs any matters left to sort out on the way to Whitesails, I‚Äôll be helping with those... but I wonder what I‚Äôll do after that.‚Äù

I‚Äôd reached a good stopping point for this area. I felt confident saying that I‚Äôd achieved the task my god had charged me with here. In order to run what looked a lot like a military campaign in Beast Woods, I‚Äôd ended up becoming a knight, but I didn‚Äôt have any land and it wasn‚Äôt a hereditary knighthood, either. It was pretty much an honorary position. Not only did I have no territory to rule over, I didn‚Äôt even hold any real official post.

I thought it might be an idea to get permission from the Duke of Southmark, if I could, and try going on a journey traveling around places like my parents had. Each day an adventure spent searching for ancient ruins and the like. That sounded fun‚Äî

‚ÄúWell, you‚Äôre gonna be the lord, aren‚Äôt you?‚Äù

...Huh?

‚óÜ

‚ÄúThe lord?‚Äù

‚ÄúThe lord.‚Äù

‚ÄúOf where?‚Äù

‚ÄúThe villages of Beast Woods.‚Äù

‚ÄúHahah.‚Äù I was worried for a moment, but Menel just had the wrong idea. ‚ÄúCome on, Menel. I‚Äôm a knight with no land who can‚Äôt pass on his title. It‚Äôs kind of like an honor, that‚Äôs all. In practice, I‚Äôm seriously no different from an adventurer!‚Äù

When I said that, not just Menel, but everyone around me went silent. Wh-What?

‚ÄúHe doesn‚Äôt realize...‚Äù

‚ÄúYou can‚Äôt be serious.‚Äù

‚ÄúReally?‚Äù

‚ÄúHe... hadn‚Äôt been thinking about it?‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd here I was, thinking he was doing a pretty good job...‚Äù

A small commotion started among the adventurers.

‚ÄúThis guy‚Äôs smart, but sometimes he‚Äôs the world‚Äôs greatest idiot, isn‚Äôt he?‚Äù

‚ÄúI can‚Äôt believe he‚Äôs so good at managing what‚Äôs under his nose, but he wasn‚Äôt even thinking about how it‚Äôd end up.‚Äù

‚ÄúNo way...‚Äù

‚ÄúSo he‚Äôs clueless...‚Äù

H-Huh?

As I stood there, puzzled, Menel sighed deeply and began to speak. ‚ÄúEven if you aren‚Äôt given land, you have the freedom to conquer unclaimed areas, don‚Äôt you?‚Äù

‚ÄúConquer? No, I don‚Äôt plan on doing anything like that.‚Äù Maybe back when I fell into that dark place where I was trying to do everything myself, but certainly not now. I didn‚Äôt want to be a ruler...

‚ÄúLemme ask you something.‚Äù

‚ÄúOkay.‚Äù

‚ÄúSay you‚Äôre someone who wants to start a business in these woods. Who do you go talk to?‚Äù

Well... That would be the person who‚Äôs started dealing with large transactions and is already working industriously to help me with just that sort of thing, namely‚Äî

‚ÄúTonio.‚Äù

‚ÄúAnd the majority of Tonio‚Äôs capital is financed by you, right? If you pull your funding, Tonio gets hit hard.‚Äù

Yep... Hm?

‚ÄúI don‚Äôt plan on doing that.‚Äù

‚ÄúBut you could, is the issue, right?‚Äù

Everyone else nodded.

‚ÄúApart from that, you have the blessing of the god of the flame and you‚Äôre a paladin sanctioned by the Kingdom and the temple. You‚Äôre authority incarnate! You have the military power, hiring all these adventurers. Oh, and you‚Äôre also arbitrating village disputes, so this place is literally your jurisdiction for all intents and purposes. And, as I just said, you also have control over goods distribution.‚Äù

I tried calmly to think of a counterargument. But... Huh? Wait... Wait, what? It was strange... I couldn‚Äôt think of... anything...

‚ÄúBasically, in the process of wiping out the demons, you‚Äôve effectively taken complete control of this region‚Äôs authority, military power, legal system, and the distribution of all its goods. The position is blatantly about to be yours, and we‚Äôre all behind you. If you suddenly renege on it with ‚ÄòI don‚Äôt want to do it‚Äô there‚Äôs gonna be chaos, brother.‚Äù

My mouth hung open. Menel and all the others were looking at me with faces of disbelief.

‚ÄúSo you literally hadn‚Äôt realized?‚Äù

I could feel myself going pale as I nodded repeatedly back.

I‚Äôd simply been hoping that if I just did a little something about the demons and then improved the poverty situation a bit, the rest would just naturally work itself out, and I hadn‚Äôt really thought in any detail about what would come after. I‚Äôd just been vaguely thinking that I could saunter off into the sunset like my parents had.

‚ÄúUhhh...‚Äù

‚ÄúYeah?‚Äù

‚ÄúWh-What do I do?!‚Äù

In the end, those were the words that came out.

This wasn‚Äôt how it was supposed to go... When my parents had solved issues like this, they‚Äôd departed the scene dashingly without any problem! Had I somehow screwed up right near the beginning, and I hadn‚Äôt even noticed?! Where?!

‚ÄúI... I guess I have to... get some people? Umm... um...‚Äù

‚ÄúYou already have merchants and priests who know about law, don‚Äôt you? If you need anyone else, I‚Äôm sure you can get that duke to introduce someone to you.‚Äù

The bishop had apparently seen this development coming. I could understand the priests who knew about the liturgy and sermons, but now I understood why the bishop had given me people with experience in law and office work as well...

‚ÄúOh my God...‚Äù

I hadn‚Äôt realized just how big things had become. As I held my head, the people around me cheered and laughed at my reaction.

‚ÄúWhere did I go wrong for things to turn out this way...? Please, tell me, Gracefeel...‚Äù

I felt like I heard faint giggling. Her as well?!

A cool wind carrying the smells of greenery and earth blew by as we laughed together in our merry circle, the dazzling summer sun shining brightly on this faraway land.

‚Äî The Faraway Paladin II: The Archer of Beast Woods ‚Äî Finis.

Afterword

To everyone who picked up this book, thank you very much, and to those who have been with me since Volume 1, it‚Äôs a pleasure to see you again. Kanata Yanagino here.

Four months after the first volume, I‚Äôve been fortunate enough to be able to publish a second. It‚Äôs thanks to all of you who pick up my books. Thank you very much.

The text of this second volume is a revised version of what I posted on Shousetsuka ni Narou one year ago. In fact, I am finishing my revisions as I write this.

Looking back on myself a year ago, I remember that I was terribly agitated, confused, and frightened. The reason was obvious: it was because my environment was changing so drastically.

The parts corresponding to Volume 1, which I had posted as practice, became the subject of some discussion. I became listed in the site‚Äôs ranking tables. More and more people started posting their impressions. There was even talk of turning it into a book. The practice work which I simply assumed that people wouldn‚Äôt take to was treated to a reception far greater than I‚Äôd ever been expecting. I was both thrilled and shocked.

Because it was so popular, feelings of self-doubt and a fear of disappointing people welled up in my heart. Would I be able to continue writing stories of similar quality? I had to choose whether to go ahead with the book idea, not go ahead with it, or put the idea on hold; and I also had to consider how I could balance it with my current job. I couldn‚Äôt even have imagined these kinds of worries back when I was writing Volume 1 and overreacting at every little comment.

Of course, at the time, I didn‚Äôt have the right mentality to deal with a situation like that. I was overthinking everything and getting excited and depressed over the smallest things. Looking back, I‚Äôm now aware that I was pretty difficult to deal with.

I am very grateful for my creative associates, who were so patient with me during that period. They lent an ear to my confused and incoherent rambling. They gave me advice on my drafts. They took part in our usual random chats. And above all, they showed me their own works, always created at their own pace. All of us chatting night after night about each other‚Äôs works was a pleasure with no substitute. By looking at what my friends‚Äô creations were like, I was able to continue typing away at my keyboard, even though I still felt very unconfident. This story, which started from a small idea, continues today thanks to the passion and enthusiasm I was able to borrow from them. For that, I am still truly grateful.

However, despite this, my writing was pretty rough due to the psychological state I was in. After I decided to revise the story for publication and looked back over it, I found something I thought I could have done a little better on everywhere I looked, and I had a real headache thinking about how to fix everything and fit it into a single volume. What to leave and in what form, how to change things, how to include new elements... Remaking something once it‚Äôs already finished is quite difficult. Unlike Volume 1, which came out very well in that sense, the second volume was a real struggle. I did everything in my power to improve it, and now, it‚Äôs like a completely new work. I sincerely hope you enjoyed it.

Finally, some acknowledgments.

To Kususaga Rin-sensei, who added marvelous illustrations to this book: Thank you, thank you, thank you. I jump for joy every time I lay eyes on one of your beautiful illustrations.

To my fellow creators: I said enough, no more, too embarrassing. Thank you, please keep on being my friends.

To my editor, the editors at Overlap, everyone involved with this book‚Äôs printing, sales and marketing, and everything else related, and to you, the person who took this book into your hands: I thank you from the bottom of my heart.

The next volume will either be The Lord of the Rust Mountains, a journey of adventure about the extermination of an evil dragon, which enjoyed the same kind of good reviews as The Boy in the City of the Dead when I published it on the web, or it will be a newly written tale of adventure which I will create specifically for book publication.

Either way, I will do my best to make it something you will all enjoy.

I hope you will read it when the time comes.

Praying that we can meet again,

Kanata Yanagino, June 2016

Bonus Interview with Kanata Yanagino

We asked the author your questions and a few of our own!

Q. If you were to be reborn and could only train with Gus, Mary, or Blood, who would you choose?

This question got me thinking... and the next thing I knew, ten whole minutes had passed!

I spent a long while going back and forth between the three of them, thinking things like: If I was going to be living in a dangerous world of fantasy, surely it would have to be Blood... Ah, but wait, knowledge is important too, and my intellectual curiosity would be satisfied as well if I went with Gus... But training with either of those two seems like it would be really hard, so maybe Mary...

However, in the end, I think my answer is Blood, because I have a lot of admiration for strong and brave warriors of fantasy like Conan the Barbarian. I‚Äôm extremely far removed from those muscular warriors, but if I were reborn, I think I‚Äôd like to try becoming someone like that.

Who would you like to train with?

Q. Which race in The Faraway Paladin are you most interested to write about? Are there any plans for entirely unique, never-before-seen races for the fantasy genre? I feel like the Genre is in a bit of a rut for racial creativity intrinsic to the fantasy genre. Also, will any character ever be as great as Meneldor!? You‚Äôve set a super high standard with him!

I like dwarves.

Brawny arms! Thick chests! Bushy beards and bright smiles! Warriors of the mountains, skilled at smithing! And I wrote the heck out of them in Volume 3. I feel very satisfied right now.

I don‚Äôt have any plans to depict races of my own creation at the moment, but it is something I‚Äôd like to try my hand at one day, as long as I‚Äôm writing fantasy novels. The great creations of hobbits, elves, and dwarves left to us by Professor J.R.R. Tolkien are very easy to use, but I think it‚Äôs important to ‚Äúfly the nest.‚Äù

And thank you very much; Meneldor is a character I‚Äôm very fond of as well. I may have set the bar high, but I will jump it, just watch me!

Q. There seems to be a great deal of devotion in this book, are you spiritually minded or is that part of the characters‚Äô motivation strictly fiction?

When I was younger, there was a period when I had a strong interest in spirituality and the teachings of traditional religions. During that time, I read all kinds of books relating to gods and Buddha, and experienced meditation for myself a number of times. So the depictions of spirituality and devotion in the book are not strictly fiction, but based on a small amount of actual experience. The things Blood talked about regarding fighting, too, contain some elements of martial arts that I studied a long time ago.

Of course, not everything in there about devotion and martial arts comes from real experience, but knowing even a little about a subject makes it that much easier to write about.

Q. How many hours a day do you write? About how long does it take to finish a volume?

Writing novels is a side job which I have to balance with my main job, so there are some days when I don‚Äôt write at all and others when I write an awful lot. As a rough estimate, I‚Äôd say about three hours per day. The story itself, I‚Äôm constantly thinking about whenever I have a free moment. The time I spend writing is many times shorter than the time I spend every day dreaming up the story. I bring it to life in my head, and turn it into writing as a finishing touch. When my imagination is working well, the writing flows easily... and when it isn‚Äôt, it can be hell!

It takes two to three months in the best case for me to finish up a volume, and sometimes longer. Some people are fast at this, and those people can be really fast. I‚Äôm a little envious of them.

Q. If you found yourself transported to the world of Faraway Paladin, to which deity would you pledge loyalty and why?

The Earth-Mother Mater, I think. She seems like the god who would show the most compassion to an unfortunate soul thrown into another world!
